Shariah's Concession for Sick Person: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Explanation of Shariah's special concession allowing a sick person to transact in one-third, overriding qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that istihsan overrides qiyas in the case of a sick person's gratuitous transactions in one-third of wealth.

Core Idea
Qiyas would forbid the sick from giving away even one-third; Shariah's mercy (istihsan) allows it.

This passage from Al-Husami illustrates a fundamental usul al-fiqh principle: sometimes the literal analogy (qiyas) would lead to a harsh ruling, but the Shariah provides a concession (istihsan) out of kindness. Here, qiyas would completely bar a terminally ill person from making any gift or charity, but the Shariah permits him to dispose of up to one-third of his wealth. Understanding when istihsan overrides qiyas is essential for deriving nuanced fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default qiyas ruling
The passage states that the requirement of qiyas is that a sick person should have no authority over his wealth for gratuitous acts (silat) at all, even within one-third.
Qiyas here is based on the original principle that a sick person is restricted to protect heirs' rights.
2
Recognize the istihsan exception
The Shariah, out of kindness, grants authority specifically in one-third of the wealth for gifts and charity. This is an exception to the qiyas.
Istihsan is a source of law that allows departing from analogical reasoning for a stronger reason.
3
Understand the usul principle
This case illustrates that istihsan can override qiyas when the revealed law indicates a concession, even though the analogy would dictate otherwise.
Hanafi usul holds that istihsan is a valid method of istinbat (derivation).
4
Apply to similar cases
The one-third rule is specific to death-illness (marad al-mawt). In other contexts, qiyas may apply without istihsan.
This principle is applied across many fiqh chapters, e.g., wills and gifts during marad al-mawt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مصنف یا اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قیاس کا تقضا یہ تھا کہ مریض کو ثلث مال میں بھی صلات یعنی احسان و تبرع کا اختیار نہ ہو جیسے ہبہ کرنا ، وغیرہ اسی طرح اللہ تعالیٰ کے مالی حقوق مثل زکوۃ ، صدقہ، کفارہ وغیرہ کا اختیار نہ ہومگر شریعت نے بطور شفقت کے ثلث مال میں اختیار دیدیا۔
The author (may Allah have mercy on him) says that the requirement of qiyas was that a sick person should not have authority even in one-third of his wealth for gifts and benevolence, like giving a gift, etc. Similarly, he should not have authority over the financial rights of Allah Almighty like zakat, charity, expiation, etc. But the Shariah, out of kindness, granted him authority in one-third of his wealth.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a Hanafi legal principle: the default ruling by qiyas would be that a sick person in his death-illness (marad al-mawt) is completely restricted from any gratuitous transactions (tabarru'at), even within one-third of his estate. This is because such transactions can harm the rights of heirs. However, the Shariah, by way of concession and mercy (shafaqat), allows the sick person to have authority over one-third of his wealth for gifts, charity, etc. This is an instance of istihsan overriding qiyas. The passage also notes that this concession does not extend to obligatory financial acts that are rights of Allah, like zakat and kaffarah, except as specifically allowed.

Key Terms
صلات (silat): Benevolent gifts or grants made without compensation, synonymous with tabarru'.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning: extending a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a common effective cause (illa).
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: adopting a ruling that is more suitable or equitable, often departing from qiyas for a stronger evidence or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did qiyas require regarding a sick person's authority over his wealth for gratuitous acts?
A) Full authority over all his wealth.
B) Authority over one-third of his wealth as a default rule.
C) No authority even in one-third of his wealth.
D) Authority only for obligatory acts like zakat.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a sick person to transact in up to one-third of his wealth by way of istihsan, overriding the stricter qiyas that would completely prohibit such transactions.

Jest in Irrevocable Contracts: The Hanafi Ruling and Its Proofs
Ruling on hazl in marriage, divorce, oath, and their proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the hadith on hazl to derive the ruling that jest in marriage, divorce, and oath is treated as serious, and explain the two proofs (naqli and aqli) used by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
In marriage, divorce, and oath, jest is treated as serious—the legal effect follows the cause, not the intention.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text addresses a fascinating question: if someone makes a jest in a serious legal transaction, is the transaction still binding? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on a prophetic hadith and a rational proof. We will explore how this ruling is derived from the sources and applied to various cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the passage and list the transactions for which hazl is ineffective: original marriage contract, divorce, emancipation, forgiveness of qisas, oaths, and vows.
2
Locate the naqli dalil (transmitted proof)
Find the hadith quoted in the text. Note that it mentions three matters: marriage, divorce, and oath. Some narrations add vow (nadhr).
3
Analyze the hadith’s textual category
Determine whether the hadith is 'amm (general) or khass (specific) according to Hanafi usul. The hadith enumerates three matters, but the Hanafis treat it as a general rule applicable by text implication (dalalat al-nass) or analogy to other irrevocable acts.
The hadith is considered to have 'umum in meaning even though it lists specific examples.
4
Understand the aqli dalil (rational proof)
The author argues that the cause (sabab) alone is sufficient; satisfaction with the legal effect is not required. These transactions do not admit of cancellation or delay, similar to why khiyar al-shart is invalid in them.
5
Note extensions and the derivation method
See how the author includes forgiveness of qisas and vows: either by implication of the text (dalalat al-nass) or by analogy (qiyas).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو هذلا باصل النكاح :- اور اگر میاں بیوی نے اصل نکاح میں ہنرل کیا ، اس طور پر کہ مرد نے عورت سے کہا کہ میں مذا قالوگوں کے سامنے نکاح کرونگا، اور اصل میں ہمارے درمیان نکاح نہیں ہوگا ، پھر یہ دونوں خواہ ہنرزل و مذاق پر متفق ہوں، یا مذاق کو نظر انداز کرنے پر متفق ہوں، یا ہنرل و مذاق ہونے میں دونوں کا اختلاف ہو جائے ، یا دونوں عقد کے وقت ہنرل سے خالی الذھن ہوں بہر صورت عقد لازم ہو جائے گا، اور ہنرل کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا، یہی حکم طلاق کا بھی ہے، کہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا کہ میں مذاقاً تجھے طلاق دونگا، اور حقیقت میں طلاق نہ ہوگی ، اسی طرح مولی نے غلام کو کہا میں تجھے مذاقاً آزاد کرونگا، حقیقت میں آزادی نہ ہوگی۔ اسی طرح قاتل سے مقتول کے ولی نے قصاص کو مذاقاً معاف کر دیا ، یا یمین پر ہنرل کیا، یمین سے مراد تعلیق ہے۔ یعنی بیوی سے کہا کہ میں مذاق میں تجھے تعلیقاً طلاق دونگا، یا غلام سے کہا کہ میں مذاقاً تیری آزادی کو معلق کرونگا، یامدا قا نذر مانی تو ان تمام صورتوں میں عقد لازم ہو جائے گا یعنی طلاق ، عتاق واقع ہوگا ، قصاص معاف ہوگا، قسم اور نذر واقع ہو جائے گی ،اور ہنرل کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا ۔۔اس پر مصنف دو دلیلیں ذکر کر رہے ہیں نقلی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے " ثلث جدهن وجد ، هذلهن جد النكاح والطلاق والیمین تین چیزیں ایسی ہیں کہ ان کو سچ مچ انجام دینا تو سچ سچ ہے ہی ، ان کا مذاق بھی سچ سچ پر محمول ہے، وہ تین چیزیں یہ ہیں نکاح ، طلاق ، اور یمین ، اور بعض روایتوں میں نذر کا بھی ذکر ہے، اور رہا قصاص کو معاف کرنا تو اس کا ثبوت دلالت النص سے ہے، اس طرح کہ عفو عن القصاص اعتاق کے قبیل سے ہے، اور دلیل عقلی یہ ہے کہ یہاں مذاق کرنے والا سبب سے اور اس کے تکلم سے تو راضی ہے، البتہ سبب کے حکم سے راضی نہیں ہے، اور اس میں رضا کی ضرورت بھی نہیں ہے، محض سب کا پایا جانا کافی ہے، جب سب پایا گیا تو حکم واقع ہو جائے گا، چاہے حکم پر راضی ہو یا نہ ہو، کیونکہ یہ سب معاملے ایسے ہیں جن میں نہ رد کا احتمال ہے ، نہ اقالہ اور فسخ کا احتمال ہے اور نہ تراخی کا، یہی وجہ ہے کہ ان معاملات میں خیار شرط نہیں ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ خیار شرط سے تو انعقاد سبب کے بعد حکم مؤخر ہو جاتا ہے، اور ان معاملات میں تراخی کا احتمال نہیں ہوتا ہے، پس جیسے خیار شرط ان اسباب میں مؤثر نہیں ہوتا ہے، ایسے ہی ہنرل بھی مؤثر نہ ہوگا۔
And if they jest in the original marriage contract: meaning if the spouses jest in the original marriage contract, such that the man says to the woman, 'I will marry you in front of people as a joke, but in reality there is no marriage between us,' then whether they agree on the jest and joke, or agree to ignore the joke, or they differ about it being a jest, or both are free-minded at the time of the contract, in all cases the contract becomes binding (lazim), and the jest is disregarded. The same ruling applies to divorce: if a man says to his wife, 'I divorce you jokingly,' and in reality no divorce occurs; similarly, if a master says to his slave, 'I free you jokingly,' but in reality freedom does not occur. Also if the heir of the murdered person forgives qisas (retribution) jokingly, or if he jests about an oath (yamin) — meaning a conditional statement: he says to his wife, 'I jokingly give you a conditional divorce,' or to his slave, 'I jokingly make your freedom conditional,' or he makes a vow (nadhr) jokingly, then in all these cases the contract becomes binding: divorce occurs, emancipation occurs, qisas is forgiven, oath and vow become effective, and the jest is not regarded. On this, the author mentions two proofs: the transmitted proof (naqli) is the hadith: 'Three things, their seriousness is serious and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and oath.' These three things, when done seriously, are serious, and their jest is also treated as serious. Some narrations also include vows. As for forgiveness of qisas, its proof is from the implication of the text (dalalat al-nass), since forgiving qisas is of the category of emancipation. The rational proof (aqli) is that the person jesting is satisfied with the cause (sabab) and his utterance, but he is not satisfied with the legal effect of the cause; however, satisfaction is not required; the mere existence of the cause is sufficient. When the cause is present, the legal effect occurs, whether the person is satisfied with it or not. These matters are such that they do not admit of cancellation (radd), revocation (iqalah), annulment (faskh), or delay (tarakhi). This is why the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) does not exist in these matters, because khiyar al-shart delays the legal effect after the cause, and these matters do not admit of delay. So just as khiyar al-shart is ineffective in these causes, similarly jest is ineffective.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is an istinbat (derivation of a ruling from sources). It states the ruling that when hazl (jest) occurs in the original marriage contract itself, the contract is still binding regardless of the parties' states of mind. The same applies to divorce, emancipation, forgiveness of qisas, oaths, and vows. The author then provides two proofs. First, a hadith (naqli): 'Three things, their seriousness is serious and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and oath.' This hadith directly establishes that jest in these matters is treated as serious. The forgiveness of qisas is included by analogy (dalalat al-nass) because it resembles emancipation. Second, a rational proof (aqli): The cause (sabab) of the contract itself is present, and the legal effect follows from the cause regardless of the person's satisfaction. These matters are irrevocable and cannot be delayed, similar to khiyar al-shart which is also ineffective in them. Thus, jest has no effect. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
ہنرل (ہزل) (hazl): Jest or joke in a legal contract; in these matters, legally ineffective.
جد (jidd): Seriousness; the opposite of hazl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this lesson, how many matters are such that their jest is treated as serious?
A) Two: marriage and divorce
B) Three: marriage, divorce, and oath
C) Four: marriage, divorce, oath, and vow
D) Only marriage

Key Takeaway
In irrevocable transactions such as marriage, divorce, oath, emancipation, forgiveness of qisas, and vow, the legal effect follows the cause, not the intention; jest does not affect the validity.

Istihsan — Juristic Preference
intermediate
6 min
Hukmi Transfer Under Duress: Conditions and Application
States rule and conditions for transferring coerced act to coercer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on hukmi transfer under duress by identifying its two conditions and distinguishing between transfer of the act's ruling (emancipation) and transfer of liability (damān).

Core Idea
Under complete duress, only destructive effects transfer to the coercer; speech acts remain with the coerced.

In this passage from Al-Husami, the author lays out a foundational rule of Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when an act is performed under complete duress (ikrah tam), the ruling of that act is transferred 'hukmi' (legally) from the coerced to the coercer — but only if two conditions are met. The rule is then applied to the case of coerced emancipation to show that while the utterance of the talāq belongs to the coerced, the destruction of property value (itlaf al-māliyya) belongs to the coercer. Understanding this nuance is key to correctly assigning liability in duress scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule: Hukmi Transfer
The passage begins by stating that transfer of an act under duress is a hukmi (legal) matter, not a sensory one. This means the act's ruling is attributed to the coercer while the coerced remains the physical doer. Grasp this foundational distinction.
Hukmi transfer does not change the physical act; it only changes the legal consequences.
2
List the Two Conditions for Transfer
The passage specifies two conditions for the transfer to be valid: (1) the act must be conceivable (ma'qūl wa mutaṣawwar) from the coercer, and (2) the act must not have a physical existence (wujūd ḥissī) from the coercer. Write these down and consider why each is necessary.
Condition 2 ensures the transfer is not sensory; if the coercer performed the act physically, there is no need for hukmi transfer.
3
Apply the Rule to Coerced Emancipation
The author applies the rule: if someone is forced to free a slave, the coerced speaks the emancipation, so the emancipation itself is attributed to him (not the coercer) because condition 1 fails — the coercer cannot speak through another's tongue. However, the destruction of the slave's value (itlaf) satisfies both conditions and transfers to the coercer.
This demonstrates that different aspects of a single act may be treated separately under hukmi transfer.
4
Understand the Separation of Itlāf from the Act
The passage addresses a potential objection: how can the destruction be separated from emancipation? It explains that itlāf and the act (e.g., emancipation) are not inseparable; as shown in the case of killing a slave, destruction can occur without any verbal act. Therefore, the itlāf within emancipation is separable and transfers to the coercer.
This step reinforces that transfer is not all-or-nothing; it depends on the specific aspect of the act.
5
Extract the Principle for Further Application
From this case, we derive a broader usul principle: under complete duress, liability for property destruction shifts to the coercer whenever the destruction is conceivable from him and not physically performed by him. This principle can be extended to other acts (e.g., forced sale, forced divorce) where financial consequences arise.
This principle is an example of istihsan in Hanafi thought.

واذا ثبت انه امر حكمی :- مصنف ہی بطور تمہید کے ایک قاعدہ بیان کر کے اس پر ایک مسئلہ متفرع کر رہے ہیں، تمہید یہ ہے کہ جب یہ بات ثابت ہو گئی کہ مکرہ کی طرف فعل کا انتقال امر حکمی ہے، امیرحسی نہیں ہے ، تو اس فعل کا انتقال اس وقت درست ہو گا جب دو باتیں پائی جائیں (۱) پہلی بات یہ کہ مکرہ کی طرف اس فعل کا انتقال معقول و متصور ہو ممکن ہو، کیونکہ اگر فعل معقول و متصور ہی نہ ہو تو وہ فعل ممتنع ہوگا ، لہذا اس فعل کی نسبت مکرہ کی طرف بالکل درست نہ ہوگی ، (۲) اور دوسری بات یہ کہ اس فعل کا وجود حسی نہ ہو، کیونکہ اگر مکرہ سے فعل کا صدور حشا ہوگا تو اس فعل کی نسبت مکرہ کی طرف حسی ہوگی، نہ کہ حکمی ، حالانکہ یہ بات ثابت ہے کہ مکروہ کی طرف نسبت و انتقال امر حکمی ہے نہ کہ امیر حسی۔
مصنف عالی، اس تمہید پر یہ مسئلہ متفرع کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی کو اکراہ کامل کے ذریعہ غلام آزاد کر نے پر مجبور کرنے کیا گیا، تو مکرہ ہی اعتاق کا تکلم کرنے والا ہوگا اور مکرہ کی طرف ہی اعتاق کی نسبت ہوگی حتی کہ غلام کی ولاء اسی کے لئے ہوگی، اور اس فعل اعتاق میں مکروہ کو مکرہ کا آلہ قرار دیکر اعتاق کی نسبت مکرہ کی طرف نہ کریں گے، کیونکہ نسبت و انتقال کے لئے پہلی شرط یہ تھی کہ وہ فعل مکرہ سے معقول و متصور الصدور ہو، اور یہاں یہ ممکن نہیں ہے کہ مکرہ کی زبان سے مکرہ کی طرف سے اعتناق کا صدور ہو سکے، اس لئے کہ انسان دوسرے کی زبان سے تکلم نہیں کر سکتا لیکن اعتاق کی وجہ سے مکرہ غلام کی قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا، کیونکہ عبد معتق کا اتلاف مکرہ کی طرف منسوب ہوسکتا ہے، اس میں اس کی دونوں شرطیں پائی جاتی ہیں، اس لئے کہ مالیت عبد کا اتلاف ایسا امر ہے جس کا صدور مکرہ سے ممکن ہے، متصور ہے، اور اس کا صدور حسا نہیں ہے، لہذا انتقال کی دونوں شرطوں کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے اتلاف مکرہ کی طرف منتقل ہو جائے گا، لہذا ایسا ہو جائے گا گویا مالیت کو مکر و نے تلف کیا ہے ، اور مکر و صرف آلہ ہے، پس غلام کی مالیت کا ضمان مکرہ پر آئے گا۔ لانه منفصل عنه : - ایک سوالِ مقدر کا جواب ہے، جس کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ جب اعتناق مکرہ کی طرف منتقل نہیں ہوسکتا، تو پھرا تلاف جو اعتاق کے ضمن میں پایا جارہا ہے وہ بغیر عتق کے کیسے مکرہ کی طرف منسوب ہوگا ؟ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اتلاف اور اعتاق لازم ملزوم نہیں ہیں کہ اعتاق نہ ہو تو اتلاف بھی نہ ہو، بلکہ بعض صورتوں میں دونوں ایک دوسرے سے الگ بھی ہو جاتے ہیں، جیسے کسی کے غلام کو قتل کر دیا، تو اتلاف تو ہے اعتاق نہیں ہے، اور اتلاف اپنی اصل ہی سے یعنی ابتدا ہی سے انتقال کا احتمال رکھتا ہے پس اگر کسی نے کسی کو اپنا غلام قتل کرنے پر مجبور کیا اور مکرہ نے غلام کو قتل کر دیا تو یہ قتل واتلاف مکرہ کی طرف منتقل ہوگا، مکر وصرف آلہ ہوگا، تو جب اتلاف ابتدا ہمکرہ کی طرف منتقل ہو سکتا ہے تو وہ اختلاف جو اعتاق کے ضمن میں متحقق ہوا ہے ، وہ بھی اعتماق سے جدا ہو کر مکرہ کی طرف منتقل ہو جائے گا۔
When it is established that it is a hukmi matter: The author, by way of introduction, states a rule and then derives a case from it. The introduction is that when it is established that the transfer of the act to the coerced [mukrah] is a hukmi matter, not a physical one, then the transfer of this act is correct only when two things are present: (1) First: that the transfer of this act to the coercer [mukrih] be conceivable and possible, because if the act is not conceivable and possible, it is impossible, so attributing this act to the coercer is not correct at all. (2) Second: that the physical existence of the act not be present, because if the act emanates physically from the coerced, then the attribution of this act to the coercer would be physical, not hukmi, whereas it is established that the attribution and transfer to the coerced is a hukmi matter, not a physical one. The great author, upon this introduction, derives the case that if someone is forced through complete duress to free a slave, then the coerced is the speaker of emancipation, and the emancipation is attributed to him, so much so that the wala of the slave belongs to him. And in this act of emancipation, we do not make the coerced a tool for the coercer and attribute the emancipation to the coercer, because the first condition for attribution and transfer is that the act be conceivable and possible from the coercer, and here it is not possible for the utterance of emancipation from the tongue of the coercer on behalf of the coerced to occur, because a person cannot speak through another's tongue. However, due to the emancipation, the coercer becomes guarantor of the slave's value, because the destruction of the freed slave can be attributed to the coercer, and both conditions are present in this. Because the destruction of the slave's value is something whose occurrence from the coercer is possible and conceivable, and its occurrence is not physical. Therefore, due to the presence of both conditions for transfer, the destruction transfers to the coercer. Thus it becomes as if the coercer destroyed the value, and the coerced is merely a tool. So the guarantee of the slave's value falls upon the coercer. "Because it is separate from it" is the answer to an implied question, whose formulation is: When emancipation cannot transfer to the coercer, then how can the destruction found within the emancipation be attributed to the coercer without emancipation? The gist of the answer is that destruction and emancipation are not inseparable such that if there is no emancipation, there is no destruction. Rather, in some cases they are separate from each other, such as when someone kills another's slave; there is destruction but no emancipation. And destruction, from its very origin, i.e., from the beginning, has the potential for transfer. So if someone compels another to kill his own slave, and the coerced kills the slave, this killing and destruction transfers to the coercer, and the coerced is merely a tool. Therefore, when destruction can transfer to the coercer from the beginning, then the destruction that occurs within emancipation also separates from emancipation and transfers to the coercer.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding acts performed under duress. The author explains that when a person is coerced into performing an act, the ruling (hukm) of that act is transferred from the coerced (mukrah) to the coercer (mukrih), but this transfer is hukmi (legal), not physical (hissi). Two conditions are required for this transfer: first, the act must be such that it can conceivably be attributed to the coercer (e.g., speaking, hitting); if it is impossible for the coercer to perform the act (like speaking through someone else's tongue), the transfer fails. Second, the act must not have a physical existence from the coercer; if the coercer physically performs it, the attribution is sensory, not legal. The author applies this to the case of freeing a slave under duress. The coerced speaks the words of emancipation, so the emancipation itself is attributed to him, not the coercer, because the coercer cannot speak through him. However, the destruction of the slave's property value (itlaf al-maliyya) is attributed to the coercer because it satisfies both conditions: the coercer can cause the loss of value, and it is not a physical act of the coercer. Thus, the coercer is liable for the slave's value (daman). The passage also addresses a potential objection: how can the destruction be separated from emancipation? The author explains that destruction and liberation are not intrinsically linked; killing a slave is a clear example where destruction occurs without emancipation. Therefore, the destruction in emancipation is separate and transfers to the coercer.

Key Terms
انتقال حكمي (intiqal hukmi): Legal transfer of the ruling of an act from the coerced to the coercer, not a physical transfer
آلہ (alah): Tool; the coerced is considered a tool of the coercer such that the act is attributed to the coercer
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage to property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule on duress, what are the two conditions for the hukmi transfer of an act from the coerced to the coercer?
A) The act must be sinful and the coercer must intend it
B) The act must be conceivable from the coercer and not physically performed by him
C) The coerced must be Muslim and the act must be a verbal transaction
D) The duress must be just and the act must be related to property

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi thought, under complete duress, the ruling of destructive acts transfers hukmi to the coercer, while speech acts remain with the coerced, determining liability accordingly.
